The Meaning of 'I am a Believer if Allah Wills'
Ghazali justifies the salaf's istithna and refutes an objection about faith's comparability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theological significance of the salaf's use of 'if Allah wills' in expressing faith and why it does not imply doubt but rather acknowledges variation in degrees of faith.

Core Idea
The salaf's 'if Allah wills' does not express doubt but acknowledges the varying degrees of faith among believers.

In this passage, Imam al-Ghazali addresses a common question about the expression 'I am a believer if Allah wills' used by the early Muslims (salaf). Some might think this indicates doubt in one's own faith, but Ghazali explains a deeper theological nuance: faith varies in degree, and the exception (istithna) acknowledges that one's faith is not on par with the prophets and angels. He then defends this position by discussing the nature of certainty and the concept of equivocal quiddity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and the literal translation to get the flow of Ghazali's argument.
Pay attention to the structure: an objection followed by an answer.
2
Identify the objection and answer
The objection is about istithna implying doubt; the answer distinguishes between similitude (mithaliya) and comparison (tashbih).
No one equates the faith of ordinary people with that of prophets.
3
Understand the variation in certainty
Ghazali explains that certainty varies in clarity, not in essence, using the example of the world's origination versus one-half-two.
The speed of conviction differs due to the presence or absence of premises in the mind.
4
Grasp the denial of mushakkik quiddity
Those who deny equivocal quiddity argue that variation is accidental, not part of the essence.
This supports the view that faith is one in essence but differs in degrees accidentally.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
معنى مسألة: فإن قلت: ما وجه قول السلف: أنا مؤمن إن شاء الله. والإستثناء المثلية تقتضي المساواة في كل الصفات والتشبيه لا يقتضيه فلا أحد يسوي بين إيمان آحاد الناس وإيمان الملائكة والأنبياء يتفاوت بأمور زائدة وقالوا ما يظن من أن القطع يتفاوت قوة إنما هو راجع إلى جلائه وظهوره وإنكشافه، فإذا ظهر القطع بحدوث العالم بعد ترتيب مقدماته المؤدية إليه كان الجزم الكائن فيه كالجزم في حكمنا الواحد نصف الإثنين، وإنما تفاوتهما بإعتبار أنه إذا لوحظ هذا كان سرعة الجزم فيه ليس كالسرعة التي في الآخر وهو الواحد نصف الإثنين خصوصاً مع غيبة النظر عن ترتيب مقدمات حدوث العالم عن الذهن، فيخيل أن الجزم بأن الواحد نصف الاثنين أقوى وليس كذلك، إنما هو أجلى عند العقل فهم ومن وافقهم يمنعون ثبوت ماهية المشكك ويقولون أن الواقع على أشياء متفاوتة فيه يكون التفاوت عارضاً لها خارجاً عنها لا ماهية له ولا جزء ماهية لإمتناع إختلاف الماهية وإختلاف جزئها.
Meaning of the question: If you say: What is the justification for the predecessors' saying 'I am a believer if Allah wills'? And the exception (istithna)? The similitude requires equality in all attributes, but comparison does not require it. So no one equates the faith of ordinary people with the faith of angels and prophets; it varies by additional matters. And they said: What is thought that certainty (qat') varies in strength is only due to its clarity, manifestation, and unveiling. When certainty of the world's origination appears after arranging its premises leading to it, the conviction (jazzm) inherent in it is like the conviction in our judgment that one-half-two [sic], and the difference between them is only considered that if one observes this, the speed of conviction in it is not like the speed in the other, which is that one is half of two, especially with the absence of consideration of the arrangement of the premises of the world's origination from the mind. So one imagines that conviction that one is half of two is stronger, but it is not so; it is only clearer to the intellect. They and those who agree with them deny the existence of a 'mushakkik' (equivocal) quiddity (mahiya) and say that what applies to varying things: the variation is accidental to them, external to them, not part of the quiddity or a part of the quiddity, because the quiddity and its part cannot differ.
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. Ghazali poses a question: what is the meaning of the salaf's phrase 'I am a believer if Allah wills' (istithna)? The objection is that if faith is one thing, saying 'if Allah wills' suggests doubt. The answer: The word 'similitude' might imply equality, but 'comparison' (tashbih) does not. No one compares the faith of common people to that of angels and prophets; faith varies by additional matters. They explain that the variation in certainty (qat') is not in its strength per se but in its clarity, manifestation, and unveiling. For example, the certainty that the world is originated (hadith) after arranging premises is not intrinsically weaker than the certainty that one is half of two; the latter appears faster because the premises are not consciously considered. So the difference is in clarity (jala') to the mind, not in the essence of certainty. Those who deny mushakkik quiddity argue that when a concept applies to varying things, the variation is accidental and external, not part of the quiddity or its parts, because the quiddity itself cannot differ.

Key Terms
إستثناء (istithna)
مثلية (mithaliya)
تشبيه (tashbih)
قطع (qat')
جزم (jazzm)
ماهية مشككة (mahiya mushakkika)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The salaf's 'if Allah wills' indicates doubt in one's faith.
Correction: It actually acknowledges the varying degrees of faith, not doubt, and no one equates their faith to that of prophets.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what does the istithna 'if Allah wills' signify in the statement of the salaf?
A) Doubt about one's own faith.
B) Acknowledgment that faith varies in degree among individuals.
C) Fear of hypocrisy.
D) A linguistic convention with no theological meaning.

Key Takeaway
The salaf's istithna does not express doubt but highlights that faith can vary in degree, and certainty (qat') differs in clarity, not in strength.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'I am a believer' or 'Masha'Allah', is it ever with a hidden sense of pride in your own faith? How does knowing that the salaf said 'if Allah wills' out of humility (not doubt) challenge you to examine the intentions behind your words? This week, before you speak about your faith, pause and ask Allah to keep you from self-amazement.
?
The passage contrasts certainty that the world is created with certainty that one is half of two. The latter is clearer because its proof is always present. Think of a core Islamic belief you hold—like the existence of God or the truth of the Quran. Is that certainty always clear and present in your mind, or does it fade when you are distracted? What specific steps can you take to make that reality as clear as 2+2=4 in your daily life?
?
Ghazali says variation in faith is accidental, not essential—meaning your faith can increase or decrease in its effects and clarity without changing its true nature. Have you ever felt that your faith has 'left' you during a trial? How does this teaching help you hold onto the hope that the essence of your faith remains, and that you can work on the accidental aspects? Commit to one action today that will bring more clarity to your faith, such as reflecting on a verse of the Quran or making dhikr.

Faith Increases and Decreases: Evidence from Hadith
Imam Ghazali argues that faith can increase and decrease, citing hadith with varying measures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the varying measures of faith in the hadith prove that faith can increase and decrease.

Core Idea
Faith's measures in hadith show it can be more or less.

This passage presents a key proof from the Prophetic hadith that faith is not a fixed quantity. Imam Ghazali argues that if faith were uniform, the different weights mentioned in the reports would be meaningless. The variation in measures demonstrates that faith can be deficient and increase, affirming the doctrine of faith's variability.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the literal translation. Notice the rhetorical question at the end.
The passage is concise but packed with meaning.
2
Identify the two hadith measures
The passage mentions two measures: a grain-weight and a dinar-weight. Understand what each represents in terms of magnitude.
A grain is very small, a dinar is larger.
3
Understand the logical inference
Ghazali asks: if faith never varies, why would the hadith specify different amounts? The difference implies variation.
This is a simple but powerful argument.
4
Reflect on the doctrinal significance
Consider why this matters: if faith can increase and decrease, then believers must strive to increase it through worship and good deeds.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإيمان ونقصانه حق ، وكيف لا وفي الأخبار أن يخرج من النار من كان في قلبه مثقال ذرة من إيمان. وفي بعض المواضع في خبر آخر مثقال دينار فأي لاختلاف مقاديره إن كان ما في القلب لا يتفاوت؟
Faith and its deficiency are true. How could it not be, when in the reports (there is) that he will come out of the Fire who has in his heart a grain-weight of faith? And in some places in another report, 'a dinar-weight'. So what is the meaning of the difference in its measures if what is in the heart does not vary?
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the position that faith can increase and decrease. Imam Ghazali states that faith and its deficiency are real (not just metaphorical). He asks rhetorically how could it not be, given the hadith reports that a person will exit the Fire if they have even a grain-weight of faith in their heart. Another report mentions a dinar-weight. The variation in the measures (grain vs. dinar) indicates that the amount of faith in the heart is not uniform. If faith were invariable, the difference in measures would be meaningless. Thus, the evidence from the differing hadith proves that faith can vary.

Key Terms
الإيمان
نقصان
مثقال ذرة
مثقال دينار
تفاوت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Faith is a single indivisible entity that cannot vary.
Correction: The hadith mention different weights (grain vs. dinar), indicating that faith can be more or less, not simply present or absent.

Checkpoint

A) That faith is purely metaphorical.
B) That faith can vary in amount.
C) That only perfect faith saves one from Hell.
D) That the reports are contradictory.

Key Takeaway
The varying measures of faith in the hadith demonstrate that faith is not a fixed quantity but can increase and decrease.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Faith and Disbelief

Defending the Exception Phrase in Declaring Faith
Ghazali explains and defends the salaf's use of 'in sha Allah' in declaring faith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the salaf used the phrase 'in sha Allah' when declaring faith, and how Ghazali defends this practice against the objection that it implies doubt.

Core Idea
Faith is certain, but its degrees vary; 'in sha Allah' acknowledges that true reality is known to God.

When the righteous predecessors (salaf) said 'I am a believer, if God wills,' some critics argued that this exception implies doubt. Yet the salaf were not uncertain about their faith; they were acknowledging that faith can increase and decrease. In this passage, Ghazali explains the deeper rationale, using the analogy of certainty and the nature of essence to show that the exception is valid and does not undermine faith's certainty.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Ghazali uses a very compressed argument here.
2
Identify the objection
The passage opens with an objection: what is the meaning of the salaf saying 'I am a believer, if God wills'? The objector thinks the exception (istithna) implies doubt or comparison that requires equality.
Understand why using 'if God wills' might be seen as problematic.
3
Understand the first part of the answer
Ghazali responds that faith varies in strength (fada'il), so no one equates the faith of common people with that of prophets and angels. The exception acknowledges this variation, not doubt.
The exception is about degrees, not certainty.
4
Follow the analogy with certainty
Ghazali uses the example of certainty about the world's temporality (huduth al-'alam) and certainty that one is half of two. Both are certain, but one appears clearer due to mental accessibility. The difference is in clarity, not in the essence of certainty.
This analogy shows that something can be certain yet vary in apparent strength.
5
Reflect on the philosophical principle
Those who deny 'mushtarak' essence argue that difference in attributes does not affect the essence. This supports the view that faith's essence is one, and the exception refers to accidental qualities.
This is a deeper linguistic and metaphysical justification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت . ما وجه قول السلف : أنا مؤمن إن شاء الله . والإستثناء المثلية تقتضي المساواة في كل الصفات والتشبيه لا يقتضيه فلا أحد يسوي بين إيمان آحاد الناس وإيمان الملائكة والأنبياء يتفاوت بأمور زائدة وقالوا ما يظن من أن القطع يتفاوت قوة إنما هو راجع إلى جلائه وظهوره وإنكشافه ، فإذا ظهر القطع بحدوث العالم بعد ترتيب مقدماته المؤدية إليه كان الجزم الكائن فيه كالجزم في حكمنا الواحد نصف الإثنين ، وإنما تفاوتهما بإعتبار أنه إذا لوحظ هذا كان سرعة الجزم فيه ليس كالسرعة التي في الآخر وهو الواحد نصف الإثنين خصوصاً مع غيبة النظر عن ترتيب مقدمات حدوث العالم عن الذهن ، فيخيل أن الجزم بأن الواحد نصف الاثنين أقوى وليس كذلك ، إنما هو أجلى عند العقل فهم ومن وافقهم يمنعون ثبوت ماهية المشكك ويقولون أن الواقع على أشياء متفاوتة فيه يكون التفاوت عارضاً لها خارجاً عنها لا ماهية له ولا جزء ماهية لإمتناع إختلاف الماهية وإختلاف جزئها .
If you say: What is the meaning of the statement of the Salaf, 'I am a believer, if God wills'? The exception for comparison requires equality in all attributes, and likeness does not require it, so no one equates the faith of common people with that of angels and prophets, for it varies by additional matters. And they said: What is thought that certainty differs in strength is only due to its clarity, appearance, and disclosure. For when certainty about the temporality of the world appears after arranging its premises leading to it, the conviction therein is like conviction in our judgment that one is half of two. Their difference is only in that when this is observed, the speed of conviction in it is not like the speed in the other, namely that one is half of two, especially when the mind is absent from the arrangement of the premises of the world's temporality. So it is imagined that the conviction that one is half of two is stronger, but it is not so; it is only clearer to the intellect. Those who agree with them deny the existence of a 'mushtarak' essence and say that what applies to different things, the difference is accidental to them, external to them, and is neither the essence nor a part of the essence, because it is impossible for the essence and its part to differ.
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer regarding the salaf's practice of saying 'I am a believer, if God wills'. The objector questions how the exception can be valid when it might imply doubt. Ghazali explains that the salaf did not intend doubt; rather, they meant that faith varies in degrees and its ultimate reality is known only to God. The comparison of faith to certainty (qat') is used: certainty about the world's temporality and certainty that one is half of two are both certain, but they differ in clarity and speed of conviction. The difference is not in the essence of certainty but in external factors like clarity. Those who deny 'mushtarak' (equivocal) essences hold that when a term applies to different things, the difference is accidental, not part of the essence. Thus, faith's essence is one, but its degrees vary by accidental qualities.

Key Terms
الإستثناء
السلف
القطع
الجزم
الماهية المشكك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that saying 'I am a believer, if God wills' indicates doubt in one's own faith.
Correction: The salaf used this phrase to acknowledge that faith varies in strength and its ultimate state is known only to God. It does not undermine the certainty of faith; it reflects humility and reliance on God.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, why did the salaf say 'I am a believer, if God wills'?
A) Because they doubted their own faith.
B) Because they were not certain about the future of their faith.
C) Because faith varies in strength and only God knows its ultimate reality.
D) Because they were required to do so by a hadith.

Key Takeaway
The salaf's use of 'in sha Allah' in declaring faith does not imply doubt but acknowledges that faith's degrees are known only to God, and the essence of faith remains one.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'in shā' Allāh' about your faith, do you genuinely acknowledge that your certainty is variable? Or do you use it as a mere formality? What would change if you meant it deeply?
?
If the essence of faith is one but degrees vary, how does that challenge your evaluation of others' faith—especially those whose practice or conviction seems weaker than yours?
?
Ghazali says certainty does not differ in strength, only in clarity. Think of a moment when you felt your faith was 'stronger'—was it actually clearer due to reflection? What practical step can you take today to increase that clarity?

Faith and Disbelief
intermediate
6 min
Doubt in Faith is Disbelief: The Position of Sufyan al-Thawri
Ghazali condemns doubt in faith and presents Sufyan al-Thawri's strict view on declaring faith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why doubt in faith is considered disbelief according to Ghazali and how Sufyan al-Thawri's strict view on declaring faith offers a cautious path.

Core Idea
Doubt in faith is disbelief; yet even certainty must be expressed with humility.

This passage tackles a delicate issue: how should a believer declare their faith? Ghazali begins by ruling that doubting one's faith is outright disbelief. He then presents the position of the eminent scholar Sufyan al-Thawri, who warns against both overconfidence and innovation in declaring faith. As we read, we'll explore the reasoning behind this caution and its resolution in Quranic language.

Guided Path
1
Understand the prohibition of doubt
Read the opening sentence where Ghazali states that doubt in faith is disbelief. This sets the absolute boundary.
2
Examine Sufyan's two statements
Notice how Sufyan condemns two ways of declaring faith: saying 'I am a believer with God' as a lie, and 'I am a believer in myself' as an innovation.
The commentary will explain the rationale.
3
Reason through the animal analogy
Read the analogy: if asked 'Are you an animal?', one cannot say 'I am an animal if God wills'. This shows that some self-knowledge is certain and does not require exception.
4
Learn the recommended response
See how Sufyan resolves the issue by quoting the Quran: 'We believe in God and what has been sent down to us.' This shifts focus to objective declaration.
This is the takeaway for practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شك (١) ، والشك في الإيمان كفر ، وقد كانوا يمتنعون عن جزم الجواب بالإيمان ويحترزون عنه : فقال سفيان الثوري رحمه الله من قال أنا مؤمن عند الله فهو من الكاذبين ومن قال أنا مؤمن في نفسه فهو بدعة ، فكيف يكون كاذباً وهو يعلم أنه كان مؤمناً في نفسه كان مؤمناً عند الله ومن وسخياً في نفسه وعلم ذلك كان كذلك عند الله ، وكذا من مسروراً ، أو حزيناً ، أو سميعاً أو بصيراً . ولو قيل للإنسان . هل أنت حيوان لم يحسن أن يقول أنا حيوان إن شاء الله ولما قال سفيان ذلك قيل له : فماذا نقول ؟ قال : قُولُوا آمَنَّا بِاللَّهِ وَمَا أُنزِلَ إِلَيْنَا .
Doubt, and doubt in faith is disbelief. And they used to refrain from giving a definitive answer about faith and were cautious about it. So Sufyan al-Thawri, may God have mercy on him, said: 'He who says, "I am a believer with God," is among the liars. And he who says, "I am a believer in myself," it is an innovation.' So how can he be a liar while he knows that he was a believer in himself, he was a believer with God, and whoever is miserly in himself and knows that, he is likewise with God. And similarly, one who is joyful, or sad, or hearing, or seeing. And if it were said to a person, 'Are you an animal?' it would not be proper for him to say, 'I am an animal if God wills.' And when Sufyan said that, it was said to him, 'So what should we say?' He said: 'Say: We believe in God and what has been sent down to us.'
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal ruling on doubt in faith and the proper way to affirm it. Ghazali begins by stating that doubt in faith is disbelief. The early Muslims avoided giving an unqualified affirmation of faith out of caution. He then quotes Sufyan al-Thawri: One who says 'I am a believer with God' is lying, because true knowledge of one's faith belongs to God. Saying 'I am a believer in myself' is an innovation (bid'a). The reasoning is that if one knows he is a believer in himself, then he is indeed a believer with God, so the exception is unnecessary. To illustrate, if asked 'Are you an animal?', one would not say 'I am an animal if God wills' because it is inherently known. When asked what to say instead, Sufyan instructs to quote the verse: 'We believe in God and what has been sent down to us' (Quran 2:136). This shifts the focus from subjective self-assessment to objective declaration of faith.

Key Terms
شك
كفر
سفيان الثوري
جزم
بدعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that avoiding a definitive statement about faith means that doubt is permissible or that one cannot be certain of being a believer.
Correction: The caution is about claiming knowledge of God's final judgment, not about the truth of faith itself. One can be certain of one's own belief but should not assert that God has accepted it.

Checkpoint
According to Sufyan al-Thawri, what is the ruling on saying 'I am a believer with God'?
A) It is recommended.
B) It is a lie.
C) It is an innovation.
D) It is obligatory.

Key Takeaway
Faith must be affirmed with certainty, but when declaring it, we should avoid claiming knowledge of God's judgment and instead use the Quranic phrase 'We believe in God and what has been sent down to us.'

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you affirm your faith, do you do so with the implicit assumption that you know God's final judgment of you? How does this lesson challenge that assumption, and what will you change about how you speak of your faith starting today?
?
Are there areas of doubt in your heart about your religion that you have ignored, thinking they are minor or harmless? What does the opening statement 'doubt in faith is disbelief' demand of you right now?
?
If you were asked to declare your faith publicly, would your answer reflect the caution of the salaf or a careless certainty? What step will you take to ensure your words align with the Quranic response taught here?

The Use of 'إن شاء الله' in Relation to Iman: Doubt in Its Perfection, Not Its Foundation
Ghazali clarifies the meaning of 'إن شاء الله' after declaring faith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that saying 'أنا مؤمن إن شاء الله' expresses doubt about the perfection of one's faith, not its foundation, and that this is not disbelief.

Core Idea
'إن شاء الله' after declaring faith shows humility about perfection, not doubt in truth.

Imam Ghazali now addresses a common practice: Muslims sometimes say 'أنا مؤمن إن شاء الله' (I am a believer, if Allah wills). This might seem to imply uncertainty about one's faith itself. But Ghazali clarifies that this is not doubt in the fundamentals of Islam; rather, it is an acknowledgment that one's faith may not be at its highest level. This distinction is crucial for avoiding misunderstandings about the nature of faith.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note how Ghazali distinguishes between doubt in the foundation and doubt in perfection.
2
Understand the Quranic reference
Ghazali cites Quran 8:4 which describes some as 'believers truly'. Reflect on why this verse supports the idea of degrees of faith.
3
Identify the two categories
The passage mentions two divisions regarding doubt in perfection: those who are truly believers and those who are not. Think about how this applies to every Muslim.
4
Grasp the consensus
Ghazali states that only doubt in the foundation is kufr by consensus. Confirm that this aligns with mainstream Islamic theology.
5
Apply the insight
Consider how this teaching affects the way you view your own faith and how you should think about others' faith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثالث: مستندة الشك، ومعناه: أنا مؤمن حقاً إن شاء الله، إذ قال الله تعالى لقوم مخصوصين بأعيانهم: أُوْلَئِكَ هُمُ الْمُؤْمِنُونَ حَقًّا. فانقسموا إلى هذا إلى الشك في كمال الإيمان لا في أصله، وكل إنسان يرجع شاكاً في كمال إيمانه، وذلك ليس بكفر، والشك في كمال الإيمان حق من قسمين: قسم يطلق عليهم أنهم مؤمنون حقاً، وقسم لا يطلق عليهم ذلك. وليس الكفر الشك في الإيمان، وإنما الموسوم بالكفر هو الشك في أصله وثبوته للحال بالإتفاق.
The third aspect: It is based on doubt, and its meaning is: 'I am truly a believer if Allah wills,' since Allah, exalted, said to a particular people by their very selves: 'Those are the believers truly.' So they are divided by this into doubt about the perfection of faith, not its foundation. Every person returns doubtful about the perfection of his faith, and that is not disbelief. And doubt in the perfection of faith is correct from two divisions: a division of whom it is said that they are truly believers, and a division of whom that is not said. And disbelief is not doubt in faith; rather, what is marked as disbelief is doubt in its foundation and its present state by scholarly consensus.
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage deals with the third usage: using 'إن شاء الله' when claiming to be a believer (e.g., 'أنا مؤمن إن شاء الله'). The author clarifies that this does not indicate doubt about the existence of Allah or the truth of Islam, but doubt about the perfection of one's faith. The Quranic verse (8:4) distinguishes true believers from others, implying degrees. Every Muslim should be uncertain about whether they have attained that complete level. This doubt is not kufr; only doubting the foundation of faith is kufr. The author also divides people into two categories: those who are truly believers (the ones described in the verse) and those who are not. Consensus supports this distinction.

Key Terms
إيمان
كمال
شك
كفر
حق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Saying 'أنا مؤمن إن شاء الله' implies disbelief or doubt in Allah's existence
Correction: The phrase indicates doubt in the perfection of one's faith, not its foundation. Doubting the foundation is kufr, but doubting perfection is not.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what kind of doubt is expressed by saying 'أنا مؤمن إن شاء الله'?
A) Doubt in Allah's existence
B) Doubt in the perfection of one's faith
C) Doubt in the truth of Islam
D) Doubt in the Prophet's mission

Key Takeaway
Saying 'أنا مؤمن إن شاء الله' is an expression of humility about one's level of faith, not a denial of faith itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'أنا مؤمن إن شاء الله', do you truly intend humility about your faith's perfection, or do you say it as a mere habit without reflection? How can you make this expression more sincere?
?
How often do you examine your own faith to see if it meets the standard of the 'true believers' described in the Quran? Are you content with being a generic Muslim, or do you strive for the perfection that Ghazali speaks of?
?
Is there a part of your heart that fears that doubting your faith's perfection might somehow weaken it? What would it take for you to embrace the humility of saying 'إن شاء الله' without feeling defensive?

Faith and Disbelief
intermediate
6 min
The Third Face: Doubt in Perfection of Faith
Ghazali defines the third face regarding the statement 'I am a believer if Allah wills'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between doubt in the foundation of faith and doubt in its perfection, and why the expression 'if Allah wills' can signify seeking blessing and politeness rather than genuine uncertainty.

Core Idea
Doubt in the perfection of faith is not disbelief; it is a sign of humility.

In this passage, Ghazali addresses a subtle issue regarding the statement 'I am a believer if Allah wills.' Some might think that saying 'if Allah wills' indicates doubt in one's faith. Ghazali explains that the doubt here concerns the perfection of faith, not its foundation. He further clarifies that the phrase can be used for seeking blessing (tabarruk) and politeness (ta'adub), as demonstrated by the hadith of Prophet Solomon.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the structure: Ghazali first defines the third face, then explains the doubt is in perfection, then introduces an alternative meaning for 'if Allah wills'.
Pay attention to the key terms like 'kamaal' and 'asl'.
2
Identify the key distinction
Identify the two categories: doubt in the foundation of faith (asl) vs. doubt in its perfection (kamaal). Understand that Ghazali says doubt in perfection is not disbelief.
3
Reflect on the hadith evidence
Read the hadith about Prophet Solomon. Consider how the omission of 'if Allah wills' led to a lack of blessing. This illustrates the alternative meaning of tabarruk and ta'adub.
The hadith is cited to support the idea that saying 'if Allah wills' is a form of seeking blessing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثالث : مستندة الشك ، ومعناه : أنا مؤمن حقاً إن شاء الله (۲) إذ قال الله تعالى لقوم مخصوصين بأعيانهم : ( أُوْلَئِكَ هُمُ الْمُؤْمِنُونَ حَقًّا ) (۳). فانقسموا إلى هذا إلى الشك في كمال الإيمان لا في أصله ، وكل إنسان ويرجع شاك في كمال إيمانه ، وذلك ليس بكفر (٥) ، والشك في كمال الإيمان حق من قسمين وجهين : (٤) (۱) وحاصل هذا الوجه إنهم خرجوا إن شاء الله ههنا إلى معنى آخر غير الشك وهو التبرك والتأدب وإستدل عليه بالآيتين ، وحديث المقابر ومن أحسن ما يستشهد به هنا ما أخرجه البخاري عن أبي اليمان عن شعيب عن أبي الزناد عن الأعرج عن أبي هريرة رضي الله عنه أنه سمع النبي قال : قال سليمان عليه السلام لأطوفن الليلة على تسعين إمرأة كلهن تأتي بفارس يجاهد في سبيل فلم يقل إن شاء الله ، فطاف عليهن جميعاً ، فلم تحمل منهن إلا إمرأة واحدة جاءت بشق رجل والذي نفس محمد بيده ، لو قال إن شاء الله لجاهدوا في سبيل الله فرساناً أجمعون الله ، قال له صاحبه : إن شاء الله
The third face: it is based on doubt, and its meaning: I am a true believer if Allah wills (2) when Allah said to a people specifically designated: 'Those are the believers truly' (3). So they are divided into this: doubt in the perfection of faith, not in its origin, and every person returns doubting the perfection of his faith, and that is not disbelief (5), and doubt in the perfection of faith is true from two divisions and two faces: (4) (1) The summary of this face is that they took 'if Allah wills' here to another meaning other than doubt, and it is seeking blessing and politeness, and they adduced evidence for it with the two verses and the hadith of graves. Among the best evidence cited here is what al-Bukhari narrated from Abu al-Yaman from Shu'ayb from Abu al-Zinad from al-A'raj from Abu Hurayra (may Allah be pleased with him) that he heard the Prophet (peace be upon him) say: Solomon (peace be upon him) said: 'I will surely go around tonight to ninety women, each of them will come with a horseman who fights in the path of Allah,' and he did not say 'if Allah wills.' So he went around to all of them, and none of them became pregnant except one woman who came with half a man. By the One in whose hand is the soul of Muhammad, if he had said 'if Allah wills,' they would have all fought as horsemen in the path of Allah. His companion said to him: 'if Allah wills.'
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage defines the third face (wajh) concerning the statement 'I am a true believer if Allah wills.' It clarifies that the doubt expressed by 'if Allah wills' pertains to the perfection (kamaal) of one's faith, not to its foundation (asl). The author states that every person is doubtful about the perfection of his faith, and that this doubt is not disbelief (kufr). Then he explains that the phrase 'if Allah wills' in this context is not actually expressing doubt but is used for seeking blessing (tabarruk) and politeness (ta'adub). He supports this with the hadith of Solomon (AS), who neglected to say 'if Allah wills' and thus only one woman out of ninety became pregnant with a half-child. The lesson emphasizes that the doubt is about the completeness of iman, not its validity, and that such doubt is permissible.

Key Terms
شك
كمال الإيمان
أصل الإيمان
تبرك
تأدب
استثناء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that any doubt in faith is disbelief.
Correction: This passage shows that doubt in the perfection of faith is not disbelief; it is a permissible expression of humility.

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the meaning of 'if Allah wills' when used in the statement 'I am a true believer if Allah wills'?
A) It expresses doubt about the foundation of faith.
B) It expresses doubt about the perfection of faith and is not disbelief.
C) It is a sign of hypocrisy.
D) It is obligatory to say it.

Key Takeaway
Doubt in the perfection of faith is not disbelief; it can be an expression of humility and seeking Allah's blessing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think about a moment when you felt your faith was perfect. Was that feeling genuine or prideful? Now, consider: if you truly believe that only Allah can grant perfection, why do you not constantly say 'if Allah wills'? Will you commit to using this phrase as a sincere plea for perfection, not just a habit?
?
If you were asked today, 'Are you a true believer?' how would you answer? Would your answer reveal a hidden pride or a humble acknowledgment of your shortcomings? What would it cost you to admit that your faith is not yet perfect?
?
Read the hadith of Solomon again. He omitted 'if Allah wills' and suffered loss. In your daily life, where are you acting as if your success is guaranteed by your own efforts? Will you begin to attach 'if Allah wills' to every planned action, trusting that Allah's will is what brings completion?
